original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    reg q1, q2;
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q1 <= d;
        q2 <= q1;
    end
    
    assign q = q2;
    
endmodule

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    wire q1, q2;
    
    assign q1 = d ^ q;
    assign q2 = d & q;
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q <= q1;

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    reg q1, q2;
    
    always @(posedge clk)
        q1 <= d;
    
    always @(negedge clk)
        q2 <= d;
    
    assign q = q1 ^ q2;
    
endmodule

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= d;

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    reg q1, q2;

    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q1 <= d;
        q2 <= q1;
    end

    assign q = q2;

endmodule

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= d;

endmodule

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q <= d;
    end

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    wire q1, q2;
    
    assign q1 = d ^ q;
    assign q2 = d & q;
    
    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= q1;
        
endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    reg q1, q2;
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q1 <= d;
        q2 <= q1;
    end
    
    assign q = q2;
    
endmodule

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    reg q1, q2;

    always @(posedge clk)
        q1 <= d;
    always @(negedge clk)
        q2 <= d;

    assign q = q1 ^ q2;

endmodule

However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= d;

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    wire q1, q2;
    
    assign q1 = d ^ q;
    assign q2 = d & q;
    
    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= q1;

endmodule

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    wire q1, q2;
    
    assign q1 = d ^ q;
    assign q2 = d & q;
    
    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= q1;

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    reg q1, q2;
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q1 <= d;
        q2 <= q1;
    end
    
    assign q = q2;
    
endmodule

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    reg q_next;

        q_next = d;
    end
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q <= q_next;
    end

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    reg q_next;
    
    always @(*) begin
        q_next = d;
    end
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q <= q_next;
    end
    
endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    reg q1, q2;

    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q1 <= d;
        q2 <= q1;
    end

    assign q = q2;

endmodule

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    reg q1, q2;

    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q1 <= d;
        q2 <= q1;
    end

    assign q = q2;

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    wire q1, q2;
    
    assign q1 = d ^ q;
    assign q2 = clk ^ q1;
    
    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= q2;

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    reg q_next;
    
    always @(*) begin
        q_next = d;
    end
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q <= q_next;
    end
    
endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    reg q1, q2;

    always @(posedge clk)
        q1 <= d;

    always @(negedge clk)
        q2 <= d;

    assign q = q1 ^ q2;

endmodule

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q <= d;
    end

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    wire q1, q2;
    
    assign q1 = d ^ q;
    assign q2 = d & q;
    
    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= q1;

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    reg q1, q2;
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q1 <= d;
        q2 <= q1;
    end
    
    assign q = q2;
    
endmodule

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= d;

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    reg q1, q2;

    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q1 <= d;
        q2 <= q1;
    end

    assign q = q2;

endmodule

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= d;

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    wire q1, q2;
    
    assign q1 = d ^ q;
    assign q2 = d & q;
    
    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= q1;

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    reg q_next;
    
    always @(*) begin
        q_next = d;
    end
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q <= q_next;
    end
    
endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    reg q1, q2;

    always @(posedge clk)
        q1 <= d;
    always @(negedge clk)
        q2 <= d;

    assign q = q1 ^ q2;

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    wire q1, q2;
    
    assign q1 = d ^ q;
    assign q2 = d & q;
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q <= q1;
    end
    
endmodule

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    reg q1, q2;
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q1 <= d;
        q2 <= q1;
    end
    
    assign q = q2;
    
endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    reg q_next;
    
    always @(*) begin
        q_next = d;
    end
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q <= q_next;
    end
    
endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    reg q1, q2;

    always @(posedge clk)
        q1 <= d;
    always @(negedge clk)
        q2 <= d;

    assign q = q1 ^ q2;

endmodule

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= d;

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    wire q1, q2;
    
    assign q1 = d ^ q;
    assign q2 = clk ^ q1;
    
    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= q2;

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    reg q1, q2;
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q1 <= d;
        q2 <= q1;
    end
    
    assign q = q2;
    
endmodule

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

        q <= d;

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    reg q1, q2;

    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q1 <= d;
        q2 <= q1;
    end

    assign q = q2;

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    wire q1, q2;
    
    assign q1 = d ^ q;
    assign q2 = d & q;
    
    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= q1 | q2;
    
endmodule

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    wire q1, q2;
    
    assign q1 = d ^ q;
    assign q2 = d & q;
    
    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= q1 | q2;
    
endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    reg q1, q2;
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q1 <= d;
        q2 <= q1;
    end
    
    assign q = q2;
    
endmodule

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

        q_next = d;
    end
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q <= q_next;
    end

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    reg q_next;
    
    always @(*) begin
        q_next = d;
    end
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q <= q_next;
    end
    
endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    reg q1, q2;

    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q1 <= d;
        q2 <= q1;
    end

    assign q = q2;

endmodule

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    reg q1, q2;

    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q1 <= d;
        q2 <= q1;
    end

    assign q = q2;

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    wire q1, q2;
    
    assign q1 = d ^ q;
    assign q2 = d & q;
    
    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= q1;
        
endmodule

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    reg q1, q2;
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q1 <= d;
        q2 <= q1;
    end
    
    assign q = q2;
    
endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    reg q_next;
    
    always @(*) begin
        q_next = d;
    end
    
    always @(posedge clk) begin
        q <= q_next;
    end
    
endmodule

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

        q <= d;

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    reg q1, q2;

    always @(posedge clk)
        q1 <= d;
    always @(negedge clk)
        q2 <= d;

    assign q = clk? q1 : q2;

endmodule

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);

    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= d;

endmodule

original text:  // You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    wire q1, q2;
    
    assign q1 = d ^ q;
    assign q2 = d & q;
    
    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= q1;

endmodule

// You're familiar with flip-flops that are triggered on the positive edge of the clock, or negative edge of the clock. A dual-edge triggered flip-flop is triggered on both edges of the clock. However, FPGAs don't have dual-edge triggered flip-flops, and always @(posedge clk or negedge clk) is not accepted as a legal sensitivity list.

// Build a circuit that functionally behaves like a dual-edge triggered flip-flop

// Hint: You can't create a dual-edge triggered flip-flop on an FPGA. But you can create both positive-edge triggered and negative-edge triggered flip-flops.
// This problem is a moderately difficult circuit design problem, but requires only basic Verilog language features. (This is a circuit design problem, not a coding problem.) It may help to first sketch a circuit by hand before attempting to code it.

module top_module (
    input clk,
    input d,
    output q
);
    
    wire q1, q2;
    
    assign q1 = d ^ q;
    assign q2 = d & q;
    
    always @(posedge clk)
        q <= q1;

endmodule
